```

"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
news:ds7pnl0gc7@news4.newsguy.com...
>
> there are simply going to be cases where a component [...]
> has to change because of a change in requirements that can't be
> captured by specialization (at least not in a sensible fashion).
>
[...]
>
> consider a calendar component which has rules for
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> requirements which can't be anticipated by a trivial component.

    I think that as compiler-technology progresses, this could be done via 
specialization. When a government decides to change the way DST works, they 
usually do so by announcing changes to the existing system. Your 
specialization would just be encoding the changes announced as a wrapper 
around the existing component.

    When your component starts getting bloated from several layers of 
amendments, the (JIT) optimizer could kick in and simplify the code at 
compile time or at run time.

    The big issue here is that to make sure the optimizer can do its job 
properly, the programmer has to try to make their components as free of side 
effects as possible. Outputting to console is a big one: if the old code 
outputs to console, the new optimized code will have to output the same 
thing under the same conditions. What this basically means is that code 
which outputs things to console are typically harder to optimize that code 
which doesn't, and so it becomes less desirable to extend a component in 
which there exists a codepath where something gets output to the console.

>> The only time I would consider
>> modifying component code is while it is being tested and debugged.
>
> How do you know when it's free of bugs?

    When it passes all of its unit tests? One could take the stance that if 
the component doesn't do what the client wants, but passes all the unit 
tests anyway, then that's a flaw in the design of the component, rather than 
a flaw in the code of the component itself.

    As a programmer, I spend part of my time designing interfaces and part 
of my time implementing those interfaces. With component-based programming, 
I guess I would be spending more time on the interfaces (making sure they do 
exactly what the client wants), and less time on implementation (ideally, 
never maintaining it).

    - Oliver
```

```
This is EXACTLY the approach I have adopted. It works fine.
>

> amendments, the (JIT) optimizer could kick in and simplify the code at 
> compile time or at run time.

>    The big issue here is that to make sure the optimizer can do its job 
> properly, the programmer has to try to make their components as free of 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

I handle this by using a property to state where the component will run 
(Client or Server). If it is on a server, the GUI messaging is overridden 
and written to the system log instead. Interactive processes (Methods) will 
not be activated if the component is on a server.

The whole presentation layer is arguable, with different people and 
organisations using different technologies. We are using Ajax and XML. The 
jury is still out as far as I'm concerned. Some of our business favour 
desktop, others favour web based; it is a sticky wicket that will require 
intensive investigation and tool selection.

Where does that leave the components? The Properties that would be set by 
interaction are preset to default, or the invoking components set them.Most 
of the time, interactive components are in the presentation layer anyway. I 
am not an evangelist of thin client and will put components anywhere that 
makes sense.  Of course, many components don't have GUI or CLI interfaces 
anyway as they are simply called by other components.

I'm currently looking at XML for the GUI for my personal stuff, and this 
means components that want to write to the Web simply write to an XML 
buffer.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2006-02-07T17:14:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dsakii01g7u@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <S9SBf.194761$OU5.25957@clgrps13> <43qn0fF1oqu69U1@individual.net> <F77Cf.110590$AP5.18493@edtnps84> <1138298486.064822.222920@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12> <1138390683.396793.322920@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net> <droi6h02jb4@news1.newsguy.com> <44bngcF1d24oU1@individual.net> <ds7pnl0gc7@news4.newsguy.com> <u5MFf.181020$km.67269@edtnps89>`

```

In article <u5MFf.181020$km.67269@edtnps89>, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> writes:
> "Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
> news:ds7pnl0gc7@news4.newsguy.com...
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> specialization.

What can't be done by specialization?  I have a component that prints
"Hello, world", and I "specialize" it to add two integers.  If I can
add new members and methods, and override old ones, then sure, I can
implement any change I want by specialization.

The question is whether that's a *sensible* use of specialization.  I
submit that, in this case, it is not.

Consider: One of the problems with DST is the "overlapping hour"
issue.  If you have a time value in the "fall back" hour, then you
need to know whether DST had gone into effect (this was the second
instance of that hour in that day) or not (this was the first
instance) in order to correctly perform various operations on that
value, such as calculating the delta between it and other time value.

That's why C's mktime, for example, takes a DST flag; it only applies
during the "fall back" hour, but in that interval it makes a 3600-
second difference.

Now, let's consider the hypothetical component.  It has a fall-back
hour on a certain date and time.  You extend it for the new rules:
as of this date, the fall-back hour moves to *this* date and time.

Now there are two ambiguous hours in the derived component.  The
derived component needs to know whether it can trust calculations
performed by the original component, or whether it needs to adjust
them for 3600 seconds, because the original component is innocent of
the change in DST.

Well, you say, perhaps we made the code that determines whether DST
is in effect itself a virtual method, and that's what we overrode
when we specialized the component.  Good foresight - but is it
possible there are any other such aspects which need to be virtual,
but aren't?

My point is that specialization is an appropriate tool in some
circumstances, but to assume it will be appropriate in all is a
very big, and dangerous, leap.  (And performance would not be my
first concern.)

> >> The only time I would consider
> >> modifying component code is while it is being tested and debugged.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>     When it passes all of its unit tests?

How do you know your unit tests have accurately tested all the
behaviors of the component in all of its possible future applications?

Unit tests typically do a poor job of covering hidden state effects,
for example.  They do a poor job of capturing broad requirements (like
resilience to malformed input).  They do a poor job of capturing
implicit requirements (particularly for security, such as resistance
to denials of service).  They do a very poor job of detecting knock-on
effects (effects on other components or systems).

An anecdote to illustrate the last, from one of Ed Nisley's columns
on embedded systems in DDJ: a vendor of home firewall appliances
decided to implement an NTP (Network Time Protocol) client, so that
his appliance would have an accurate timestamp.  So far, so good; a
correct timestamp is important for security purposes, and you don't
want to make the user have to set the time manually.  Lots of
appliances can now get the time from some source, such as TV side
channels or WVVB broadcasts, so why not your firewall?

So their engineer put together an NTP client, or used one out of a
box, and pointed it to a public NTP server at a certain US
university.  He (or she) configured it to periodically update the
time, and to retry on failure.

No doubt it passed all unit tests, because it worked precisely as
specified.

You can probably see where this is going.  Vendor sells a bunch of
these boxes.  They all sit happily in people's houses, connected to
always-on broadband connections, periodically hitting one server at
one university for the time.  More and more of them every day - this
is an off-the-shelf product sold at big box stores.  Eventually the
server gets overloaded, and some of the firewalls find their
requests timing out.  So they retry, and keep retrying until they
get an answer.

It wasn't long before these NTP queries were consuming most of the
university's entire incoming Internet bandwidth.  They had to start
blocking incoming NTP at the border to get any useful traffic in and
out - which broke all those firewall boxes, and broke anyone else
who happened to be using their NTP service.

Unit testing does not guarantee that you've completely tested a
unit; it typically leaves significant problem domains unexplored;
and it tells you very little about how a unit will behave in its
environment.

> One could take the stance that if 
> the component doesn't do what the client wants, but passes all the unit 
> tests anyway, then that's a flaw in the design of the component, rather than 
> a flaw in the code of the component itself.

I have no problem with demanding that the specification capture the
user's requirements - though as we all know, in practice this will
mean that often specifications have to be revised.  The problem is
that unit tests don't exhaustively prove compliance to specifications,
and specifications aren't the whole story anyway.

>     As a programmer, I spend part of my time designing interfaces and part 
> of my time implementing those interfaces. With component-based programming, 
> I guess I would be spending more time on the interfaces (making sure they do 
> exactly what the client wants), and less time on implementation (ideally, 
> never maintaining it).

Yes, and I agree that allowing well-specified interfaces to drive
development is a laudable goal.  Where I don't agree is that
nontrivial components can in general be ever declared finished, or
that all components can be trivial.
```

```
Converting *to* an ambiguous representation (such as DST) isn't the

sources which use the ambiguous representation - documents, people,
etc - you can't avoid this requirement in general.

unprofessional, and irresponsible.  (I realize you are not personally
advocating it.)

One of the central lessons of computer security and risk management
is that systems have emergent behaviors - they're not just the sum
of their parts.

to solve or at least allieviate certain problems.  I think we're all

Wrong Thing.
```

```

In article <bWPGf.267023$OU5.121834@clgrps13>, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> writes:
> "Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
> news:dsg26602r6f@news3.newsguy.com...
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> behaviour of having two of their arguments summed together.

Yes, but this is beside my original point, which is that saying "X
can be done by specialization" isn't interesting - anything can be
done by specialization.  Nothing about specialization guarantees that
new or overridden behaviors are relevant to the original design.

Thus when you wrote "I think this can be done by specialization", in
reference to my original example, you were trivially correct, but
it's not a useful claim.  The question (in that case, and in general)
would be whether specialization is the best way to make the necessary
change.  Often it is (and often when it isn't, that means it's time
for a new component), but my example was an argument that there are
cases where a change in requirements should affect existing users of
the components, because it applies to them as well.

That is, their requirements changed retroactively; or to be more
formally precise, they have a general requirement ("implement the
rules of DST") that remains in effect, but a derived specific
requirement ("DST requires we change the hour at times T1 and T2")
which has now been superceded.

In such a case, the old behavior is no longer applicable for any user
of the component, so the primary justification for specialization
doesn't apply.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ DST in COBOL Programs (was Re: GoTo in Java)

_(reply depth: 30)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2006-02-10T11:25:30-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<11upfpg43vurhfb@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <1138298486.064822.222920@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12> <1138390683.396793.322920@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net> <droi6h02jb4@news1.newsguy.com> <44bngcF1d24oU1@individual.net> <ds7pnl0gc7@news4.newsguy.com> <u5MFf.181020$km.67269@edtnps89> <dsakii01g7u@news1.newsguy.com> <HqoGf.184798$AP5.144580@edtnps84> <dsg26602r6f@news3.newsguy.com> <bWPGf.267023$OU5.121834@clgrps13> <dsi68u0283g@news1.newsguy.com>`

```

"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
news:dsi68u0283g@news1.newsguy.com...
[snip]
> That is, their requirements changed retroactively; or to be more
> formally precise, they have a general requirement ("implement the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> doesn't apply.

One of the features of FUNCTION CURRENT-DATE
is the difference between local time and UTC found in
the last five characters. Because UTC is a fixed reference,
any changes in local time that result from DST must be
reflected by an adjustment in the last five characters of
CURRENT-DATE. Thus, if CURRENT-DATE is fully
supported by the COBOL runtime and DST is supported
by the OS, COBOL programs could be made immune
from changes in the transition dates for DST. Or, so it
would seem.
```

```
As usual, an excellent response, Michael.

I understand your position, but it hasn't changed mine :-).

I've made a few  quick responses below, but I really don't have time to 
labour this further.

I believe in components. I don't believe in maintaining them. I don't write 
large graphics applications or gaming or simulation software; my comments 
should be interpreted purely in the context of commercial business 
processes; accounting, administration, MIS, etc.


"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
news:ds7pnl0gc7@news4.newsguy.com...

> has to change because of a change in requirements that can't be

provided some... :-)

> an image map.  Image rendering is notorious for security holes; we've

Having never written this kind fo software, I'll take your word for it. 
However, I believe if it was approached with the idea of simplification, 
encapsulation, and decomposition it has to be possible to make it better. 
Even in the most complex of engines there are sub-assemblies. I see it this 
way; I understand that you don't. I don't think you are wrong, but you have 
failed to persuade me it can't be done. :-)

  There's no reasonable
> option other than to put the (necessarily complex) processing of the
> image data format into a component and maintain it.
>

My experience has taught me that when people say "There's no alternative" or 
"It's the only way..." what they really mean is that they WANT to do it this 
way, OR they are unable to think of another way.  I've seen this countless 
times and there are invariably alternatives that weren't considered, because 
the people were too close to the problem, no discussion and brainstorming 
was done, or there was an entrenched culture of "That's the way we do things 
here" or similar.

I have adopted a Japanese proverb as the motto for my current project where 
it has been necessary to stress that several teams of people are involved 
and it is essential that the whole thing melds as one team. The proverb is: 
"None of us is as smart as all of us." I firmly believe in discussion and 
brainstorming, even on deeply technical problems. There is no room for 
technical prima donnas (or even the Project Manager :-)) to impose a 
solution without exploring all options available. If the guidelines are: 
"Simplify, encapsulate, decompose" somebody somewhere will build a less 
complex, more efficient, 'complex engine'. Just on that, I was interested to 
note in passing today in the NZ Herald technology pages, that a company here 
is building electric motors that are highly innovative. 
(http://www.nzherald.co.nz/search/story.cfm?storyid=00077DCE-ECE1-13E6-A35183027AF10240). 
I couldn't help thinking that it was a brave man who would decide to improve 
on the simple technology of an electric motor. Yet someone did. Success has 
been 10 years coming to Wellington Drive, but they have persevered and it is 
now paying off.


> For the latter, consider a calendar component which has rules for
> daylight savings time.  Either those rules can come from processing
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> are localization issues.
Well, that's only 2 options... why stop there? What about public properties 
that allow DST to be off or on and specify when it is to start and stop? 
Anyone using the engine could specify at the time  they invoke it, what 
their DST requirements were. These can be parameters or properties and can 
be passed from anywhere you like, including a command line or control 
script. It would be tempting to include the location, but I wouldn't do 
that. Simply build a 'smarter calendar'. Then wrap the location data into 
another component that invokes the smarter calendar... The smarter calendar 
would not require maintenance (unless the rotation of the Earth was to 
change :-)) Location data would be passed through the 'location handling' 
component which would also be suitably parameterised. And so on...

Personally, in the interests of standardising and stabilising interfaces, I 
don't pass parameters to components; I use public properties instead. Set 
the properties to obtain the behaviour you want, invoke the component, get 
the results from the properties it set up when you ran its methods. Stable, 
simple, maintenance free.


 Moreover, you have situations like what
> we're faced with in the US right now, with a pending change to the
> rules for all the regions which observe DST, plus an incipient switch
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

I disagree. If you build the components right they can cope with all of the 
above scenarios.

VBScript, Turing-complete or not...

Just because something CAN be done, doesn't mean you necessarily want to do 
it.

I once wrote (for a bet) a computer program that read punched cards, 
processed the data on them, and produced printed output, using only the 
logical operators AND, OR, NOT, and XOR (plus branch, load and store 
registers - and I could have done without those by cheating and building 
them in memory form the BOOLEAN instructions...). That was Turing-complete 
(it looped through the input), but I wouldn't recommend people to adopt this 
form of application development :-)

it impossible to penetrate (if that can EVER be truly achieved). Any system

quiet when it ran for months without problem. After a year, they didn't like
```

```
Although you did NOT say so, your argument could be extended a bit to
proclaim "the end of programming".   The components that would be needed
to accomplish any task, while naturally very numerous, would be nibbled
away at until very nearly all of them were written - like maybe 99% with
some specialists around to look after the few remaining ones.  Because,
as you said, it's the interface that counts, not what's under the hood:
as long as the object code of the components is compatible it dosn't
matter what language they're written in - indeed nor does it matter what
language calls them.

But this troubles me for a couple of reasons.  One is that knowledge of
the availability of components and a precise description of their
input/output/specifications/ would be necessary for their widespread
adoption to replace the corresponding re-inventing the wheel:
communications!  Maybe we'd need a "component foundation" something like
the standards groups we have now, but handling real artifacts rather
than metalanguages. But beyond that, I can't see that that the need for
new functions will ever cease to exist.  A new theory of something or
other with commercial applications would certainly need a new vocabulary
and implementations.

But - a question.  I don't fully understand how you'd cope with
un-spec'ed examples.  Suppose you have a component that calculates
payroll & deductions.  After 10 years there comes the case of someone
with 9 children under the age of 10: which the component
mis-calculates.  I think I know what you'd do to handle this: but
perhaps you'd explain?  I ask to be sure I have it right!
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<44rgt7F3jcolU1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <S9SBf.194761$OU5.25957@clgrps13> <43qn0fF1oqu69U1@individual.net> <F77Cf.110590$AP5.18493@edtnps84> <1138298486.064822.222920@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12> <1138390683.396793.322920@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net> <droi6h02jb4@news1.newsguy.com> <44bngcF1d24oU1@individual.net> <43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net>`

```

"Peter Lacey" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> <snip>
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…
> payroll & deductions.

I wouldn't, Peter. Such functionality would be built from a number of 
components.


After 10 years there comes the case of someone
> with 9 children under the age of 10: which the component
> mis-calculates.  I think I know what you'd do to handle this: but
> perhaps you'd explain?  I ask to be sure I have it right!
>

Why would the component miscalculate this? It would have been written to 
calculate all possibilities of children for all ages and years, up to say 30 
children in 29 years. The number of children and the number of years would 
be properties. Someone (Mia Farrow?) might adopt 14 children in 4 years; 
they are still valid deductions. (note that the validations of children and 
years would not be done in the deductions component; it would simply have 
its properties set with data validated by a component in the presentation 
layer, which would have applied the rules before invoking the deductions 
component.

Perhaps it is not a good example (?)

I'll address your comment regarding unspec'd requirement.

First off, the method I use to develop components does not require a spec 
anyway and you could argue that ALL (my)components are  "unspec'd".  (They 
'evolve' out of Use Cases... it's a RAD thing...) But I digress... Once the 
component is complete it has a Help file prepared that documents its 
Methods, Properties, and Events. That is the 'spec'. It does what it says it 
does.

Now let's say we need to extend what it does. Traditionally, we'd simply 
amend an existing Method or add another one. If it needs more data elements, 
we'd add a Property to it and make it public. I don't do that. Instead I 
write a new component that has the extra property (ies) and/or methods and 
use it to wrap the existing one. I now have all of the previous 
functionality, plus the new functionality under the one 'umbrella'. If 
another system or part of thie same system wants only the original 
functionality, no problem. That component still exists and still does what 
it has always done. There isn't even any need to regression test because all 
of this is encapsulated. Only the new functionality needs testing. The 
original component was written once and never maintained. The new one will 
be exactly the same. The new component has the new Methods and Properties, 
and can also access the old ones where needed. OO languages provide 
facilities to EXTEND classes (and inherit and override all their existing 
methods and properties), and there is also the facility to simply implement 
an interface to the old component, with the interface containing it's own 
methods or properties. Or, you can simply write a new wrapper component and 
let it invoke the old one if and when it needs to.

I know it is hard to imagine this but I really don't have time to explain in 
detail, and I avoid posting code here.

I'm not trying to get everyone to embrace OO COBOL (it is probably too late 
for that) and I really don't care whether people use component based 
approaches or not. I'm reporting simply what works for me.

HTH,

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 27)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-02-11T16:01:05+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4552brF50galU1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net> <44rgt7F3jcolU1@individual.net> <43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net> <dsiheg$36a$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:dsiheg$36a$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net>, Peter Lacey  <lacey@mts.net> 
> wrote:
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> child goes from US$750 to US$1000.

I would expect the interface to this component to accommodate such a change. 
In other words there would be a property or a parameter defined at the time 
the component was built which would allow a different deduction to be 
passed.

Some people would use:

invoke TaxCalcObj "childDeductions"
           USING  ws-deduction
           RETURNING payableTax
end-invoke

I don't do it that way. I don't want the interface to my component to change 
so I achieve this by the use of properties:

The property could be set at run time (as in the example below) when the 
component is invoked, or it could be set once in the factory at the start of 
the day...(Then every instantiation of the TaxCalcObject would have this 
value available to it)

invoke TaxCalcObj "SET-kidsDeductionAmt"
            USING ws-deduction
end-invoke
invoke TaxCalcObj "childDeductions"
           RETURNING payableTax
end-invoke

Doing it this way there is no change to the interface. Every time you want 
tax amount after child deductions you just invoke the component.

invoke TaxCalcObj "childDeductions"
           RETURNING payableTax
end-invoke

Defining what are invariant variables (parameters) and what are true 
variables is something that comes out of the object modelling and the Use 
Case. Any fields required for calculation that could be subject to change in 
the future, would be defined in factory and given default values.

If the whole basis for handling child deductions was changed in an 
unforeseeable way in the future, the component would be rewritten. The 
interface would remain exactly the same and everything would continue to 
work.

>
>>Are you saying that you'd still use it but wrap it in some
>>other thing that corrects the error?

No. Personally, I would never wrap or continue using a flawed component. It 
would be replaced, just like a faulty circuit board in your home movie 
system.
>
> Or, in the words of an American Express commercial several decades old...
> 'What *will* you do?'
>
I would leave home without it :-)

Pete.
```

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:dskfhd$ddh$1@reader2.panix.com...

> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> at the code level and not at the user level.

Parameters (if passed) are passed to object code. Properties (if set) are 
set in object code. The factory object (if used) generates object code of 
object instances.  No source is necessary or even (in my opinion) desirable.
>
>>In other words there would be a property or a parameter defined at the 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> now.
I didn't realize we were discussing "how it gets done now". I thought I was 
explaining a component based approach. It may well be similar to how things 
"get done now" but there are subtle (yet very important) differences.One of 
them is that source code doesn't need to be maintained. There are too many 
others to go into here.


> What happens when the calculation changes and a different formula
> gets used?

Then a new component is required. (I thought I said that...) People who were 
in the business of selling tax systems would probably provide the new 
component automatically. The interface remains the same. Major changes in 
system functionality may require new methods.  If both new properties and 
new methods were required they would be provided through a new component or 
by wrapping the old one and overriding the existing method. No attempt is 
ever made to maintain the old component, so no source code is required. It 
does what it does.

I should add that there are many sites and people who DON'T use components 
like this and DO maintain the existing components.  My experience has been 
that these are sites used to traditional programming and approaches.

I don't use those approaches and I don't maintain components. (I am not the 
only one...:-))
>
Read it again, Doc. I covered all of this in the last post. I also explained 
why a parameter DOESN'T get passed, although it could be done that way.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 28)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2006-02-14T08:17:18-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ts3v1tlteeok2taro7qj73mo659n29oo4@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net> <44rgt7F3jcolU1@individual.net> <43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net> <dsiheg$36a$1@reader2.panix.com> <4552brF50galU1@individual.net>`

```
On Sat, 11 Feb 2006 16:01:05 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>No. Personally, I would never wrap or continue using a flawed component. It 
>would be replaced, just like a faulty circuit board in your home movie 
>system.

In Olden Dayze, when we change or replace a component, we had rigorous
testing of everything that used it.   Users were involved in the
testing, and testing was the most costly part of the maintenance.

In this new world, components are supposed to be used everywhere.   If
a basic component requires maintenance we have a choice:

1.   Assume everything using it will still work fine.
2.   Don't reuse components more than you have to.
3.   Continue the old way of rigorous testing of everything that uses
it.

In real life, a mixture of #1 & #2 is what is used.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 26)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-02-11T15:36:37+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

"Peter Lacey" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> answered!  That way you would seem less dismissive.

Peter, I spent time attempting to respond to your points. How is that 
dismissive? I even went on to try and provide a good example of the case you 
were making. I don't HAVE to do this, so a little more civility if you 
please.
>
> Let me be more general, then.  Suppose a component starts giving
> demonstrably incorrect results, several years after it's been put into
> production.  Are you saying that you'd still use it but wrap it in some
> other thing that corrects the error?

I'm saying that is a hypothetical which, so far, I have not encountered in 
the real world. This despite having many components running live now for a 
number of years. To answer your question:

1. Components cannot be faulty if what they do is properly defined. (They 
either do it or they don't, and if they don't, they don't go live.)
2. IF, by some incredible stretch of the imagination, and purely for the 
sake of intellectual argument, the scenario you posit was to happen, no, I 
would NOT wrap a faulty component. It would be replaced in its entirety, 
with the whole proces of its functionality being reviewed and rewritten. 
Fortunately, these are relatively small pieces of code, so this approach is 
viable.

The problem we are having here is that you are trying to relate my component 
approach to what you do now. It is a DIFFERENT approach, conceptually and 
practically. You might as well complain to an orange because it isn't a 
pineapple.


(I don't have the vocabulary down
> pat here so I'll state it crudely).  This would apply in both cases:
> where the original component is incorrect and where the requirements
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
See comment above.

> I would agree that your moethid of development is "un-spec'ed".  But
> that's because I regard specs as something developed BEFORE any coding
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> a thorough understanding of the problem.

I know... it isn't possible without departure from the generally accepted 
SDLC (waterfall) development methodology. I use RAD, and I use RAD Use Cases 
to design and build components. If they are being purpose built, the 
person(s) who need(s) the functionality, sits in on prototyping sessions and 
we use multiple iterations to evolve the ideas and obtain the desired 
results. The code is written then and there. It is goal oriented. (Each goal 
is a deliverable from the component). If it achieves the goal it is good; if 
it doesn't, write it again or modify it, until it does. The goals can change 
as the session continues. This is a good thing; the approach is dynamic and 
highly responsive to change. At the end of it you have a component that 
achieves the desired goals. No spec. Document what it does. Test it 
thoroughly. Done. It will never be maintained; it does what it does. Later 
you may need some additional goals... fine, wrap the compent and provide 
them. If things change and you don't need some of the functionality, fine, 
don't use it. The remaining functionality is still available. It's called 
"encapsulation".


 (Spec: I must get to Toronto
> next week, by Thursday).
That is a goal.
(A spec would give details of how it was to be achieved, flights, rental 
cars, airport access, etc.)

A "program" that achieved this goal and got you delivered to Toronto by 
Thursday, would be successful; one that got you to Valparaiso on Saturday, 
would not be.

A component based approach might achieve it as follows:

C1 => delivers: preferred mode of transport (currently only air)
C2 => delivers: flight class and booking (it will obtain destination and 
arrival date/time, and may offer choices).
C3 => delivers: schedule (includes time to airport, transfers, etc.)

Later, you decide you are not too keen on flying and want to go by road. C1 
is wrapped to include road as an option. C2 is wrapped to deliver rental car 
options. Notice that you can still go by air if you want. All that has 
happened is that your choices have been extended. C3 accesses the public 
properties (session variables if web based) posted by the other classes and 
gives you a schedule.

This is a contived and unrealistic example, but it is intended to simply 
demonstrate some concepts. It is not necessarily how this problem would be 
implemented in reality.



>What you call a spec I'd call documentation,
> because it's post-facto.
Sure. But don't lose sight of what the "documentation" is intended for. 
Yours is to help maintain the code; mine is to tell you what the component 
does.

>  It is extremely important that such a thing
> exists for the benefit of others and yourself ten years from now at
> 2a.m. in an emergency, whatever we might call it.

No, Peter, it isn't like that at all. There is no requirement for 
documentation aimed at maintenance because the component will NEVER be 
maintained. Not now, not in ten years from now, not ever...

It has a Help file stating its Methods, Properties, and events. (That is ALL 
it has and all that is needed.) It does what it says it does. It cannot do 
more or less. That is why it doesn't fail in production.

 (Documentation:
> WestJet flight 666, YWG - YZZ, 8:55 a.m., Thursday).  So I
> wholeheartedly approve of that (as if you need my approval!).
>
> A further question: are "your" components used only by yourself?

No. I have traded some with other people. One of mine has 120 current users 
(that I know of). Most of them are used by me to build applications which 
are used by other people. Currently I'm not doing any because I'm too busy 
with real work, but if I do some more web programming it is likely I'll do 
some more. Programming for me, these days, is a hobby, not a living.

Pete.
```

```
impossible in whole or in the assumptions, and in any event not worth
answering, so often that I react sharply at times.  You did attempt to

issues, not yours.  If you truly found that uncivil - it wasn't meant to

> >
> > Let me be more general, then.  Suppose a component starts giving

> 2. IF, by some incredible stretch of the imagination, and purely for the

No stretch of the imagination, incredible or not, is needed.  I wouldn't
have posited it if it hadn't happened to me.  I can think of three
instances: all were programs executed at year-end or infrequently; all
had functioned properly for six or seven years before I inherited them;
all three suddenly started producing incorrect results, apparently at
random.  One was suffering from a weird and completely unanticipated
combination of circumstances which led to a (calculated) divisor of
zero; the other two failed due to inflation!  Work fields in a
calculation didn't have sufficient digits.  When the programs were
written consideration was given to the maximum values the fields would
have to contain but the maxima were eventually exceeded.  In the other
program there was a check for zero-division; since it was considered
impossible the default action was inappropriate.

Other causes are possible.  I'd say that nearly all of them are due to
unanticipated circumstances.  I would not have the hardihood to assert
that ANY method of design and implementation is guaranteed to be
error-free and absolutely complete under all possible circumstances.  I
have hubris but not that much.

consider to be incredible if not impossible: that code can be written
and tested so perfectly in the beginning that maintenance is not
necessary.  I would venture a guess that it's not impossible that there
are others following this "ng" that agree with me.  In no other way can
I take your suggestion that nobody will do maintenance of code as
meaningful.  With respect, I can't get away from decades of experience:
things will change over the course of time that will require revisiting
the code.  I am NOT questioning your methods of development.
```

- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <S9SBf.194761$OU5.25957@clgrps13> <43qn0fF1oqu69U1@individual.net> <F77Cf.110590$AP5.18493@edtnps84> <1138298486.064822.222920@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12> <1138390683.396793.322920@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net> <droi6h02jb4@news1.newsguy.com> <44bngcF1d24oU1@individual.net> <43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net> <44rgt7F3jcolU1@individual.net> <43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net> <4550tvF4vumsU1@individual.net> <43EF81EC.BD753329@mts.net>`

```
Peter Lacey wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> impossible the default action was inappropriate.

You're mixing your apples with oranges.  You're talking about *programs* 
(big, complex things) that all broke at the same time.  Components would 
not be nearly that big.  Components are like bricks - small, simple, and 
they do what they're supposed to do.  If it's faulty, you replace it.

Think of a component to add 2 numbers together.  (Yes, almost every 
programming language already has facilities to do that, but say you 
wanted to do it by summing each digit and carrying yourself.)  It takes 
two numeric arguments, and returns one.  The spec for the component 
would indicate the maximum size and precision of the input and output 
parameters, as well as other properties that can be set or gotten (i.e., 
there could be a property that indicates whether arithmetic overflow 
occurred, if there were problems with the input, etc.).

If this component could not handle the maximum value or precision you 
required, you would not use that component.  In this case, you'd rewrite 
the component to handle the larger size, without changing the documented 
interfaces.  (If you had maintained the code, you could change it - but, 
since it's so trivial, rewriting it wouldn't be a chore.)

> The problem we are having here is that you are implying something that I
> consider to be incredible if not impossible: that code can be written
> and tested so perfectly in the beginning that maintenance is not
> necessary.

Do you think you could write 10-12 lines of code (maybe up to 50, in 
COBOL) and exhaustively test it?  That's the key to components - you 
don't try to make a component that equates to the side of the house 
(lots of bricks, the mortar, the door, the windows, and that cutout with 
the metal box for the power outlet for your Christmas lights).  You have 
a brick component, a door component, a window component, a power box, 
and the mortar is the code you use to put it all together.

Component-Based Software Engineering (CBSE) is possible.  In fact, it's 
not just possible, it's great.

> I would venture a guess that it's not impossible that there
> are others following this "ng" that agree with me.

There are also those that agree with Pete.  :)  He's not on his own on 
this.  I'd love to implement more of this, and the Unisys 2200 actually 
has a nice facility to implement and swap out components, without 
requiring the utilizing program to be recompiled or relinked.

There are challenges to getting this implemented as well - the customer 
don't trust a system-wide "hot-swappable" component.  They (up to this 
point) insist on testing "their" programs with the new version of the 
component.

> Well, for the rest of us, who have programs that do fail, and do so at
> the worst time - the aforesaid 2a.m. - your "spec" would indeed be
> invaluable.  It would help eliminate the places that don't need to be
> looked at.  

Having a detailed spec means you can also test for bad data - any good 
test should.  Ensure that the *component* handles errors gracefully.  It 
*won't* fail at 2AM.  That doesn't guarantee that the glue code is coded 
to handle the results properly.
```

```
of the best guerilla fighters in the world.

And, like their American counterparts, Australians and New Zealanders were 
subject to the abuse of a vociferous minority, who never went anywhere and 
received the right to villify, through the sacrifice of others.

It has always saddened me and it does to this day.

The best we can hope for is that todays kids won't have to go anywhere. 
Nevertheless, we should respect the ones who do and did.

Pete.

>
> -- 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2006-02-15T08:44:55-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dsvlq7$1ijv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net> <droi6h02jb4@news1.newsguy.com> <44bngcF1d24oU1@individual.net> <43E7964F.BF81BD92@mts.net> <44rgt7F3jcolU1@individual.net> <43ECB3EC.69DFB5D9@mts.net> <4550tvF4vumsU1@individual.net> <43EF81EC.BD753329@mts.net> <96528$43eff52c$45491d7a$21765@KNOLOGY.NET> <45b3s5F5sd7cU1@individual.net> <9dc2b$43f15531$45491d7a$17085@KNOLOGY.NET> <45e17mF682lbU1@individual.net> <111b3$43f28b8d$45491d7a$16483@KNOLOGY.NET> <45gcgsF6k0vcU1@individual.net> <htc6v1dd2ea5anpb33s5hjpcegbquusa5n@4ax.com>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:htc6v1dd2ea5anpb33s5hjpcegbquusa5n@4ax.com...

> It doesn't make sense to me that draftees of my era were vilified for
> being in an unpopular war, but volunteers of the current era are
> praised by people who believe the war is wrong.

Volunteers of the current era didn't volunteer *for a specific cause*, they 
volunteered to *serve their country*.  I personally haven't decided whether 
the people who got us into this war were correct and justified in doing 
so -- I'm inclined to think there were problems both with appropriateness 
and justification in this case -- but the efforts and the sacrifices of the 
military men who are following the orders of their commander-in-chief and 
doing their level best to maintain order over there deserve our praise and 
support.

To take the point to a *reductio ad absurdum*:  in 1983, the US invaded 
Grenada and toppled its government.

Did I personally think Grenada, its government or its military provided a 
serious threat to the safety of the United States?  Ummm... no.

Did I, in fact, think this could be one of the silliest conflicts we've 
involved ourselves in since that Splendid Little War we fought in 1898? 
Ummm ... yes.  (I'm not as convinced today that the 1983 war was as silly as 
I thought it was in 1983, by the way, though I still feel that way about the 
Spanish-American War, having studied it in some depth).

Does that lessen the contributions the 19 soldiers that were killed in that 
invasion, or the seven thousand that participated in it over the next two 
months made to their country?  Not one bit.  It is not *their* job to 
second-guess their superior officers.  The ones that returned deserve our 
support, and the ones that didn't deserve heroes' tributes.

Personally, the idea that a Marine should suspend "Semper Fidelis", or a 
Coast Guardsman "Semper Paratus" because he didn't like the politics of his 
superior officers, and that they should somehow take the blame for the 
results of *failing* to suspend those principles on political grounds, is 
more than a little repugnant to me.

It turns my stomach that I know *so* many Viet Nam vets who are *still* 
struggling mightily with their experiences over there and with their 
treatment on return.  I cannot know their pain, but I often wish there were 
more ways I could help lift that burden from them.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Wed, 15 Feb 2006 19:41:53 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
> 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> folk.

Yes, I agree.  For years, when people have come up to me and asked "So, 
have you been overseas?" (or kids, "Do you fly airplanes?"), I'd wryly 
smile and tell them no, I can program from anywhere.

The day after I found out, my wife and I were eating at a restaurant. 
Our 11-month-old was staring at the people at the table beside us, and 
they were enjoying it - talking to him and stuff.  They happened to just 
ask the off-hand question "So, are you going overseas?"  I now have a 
different answer.

> 2.  I disagree with that article.   It is not dishonest to support the
> troops and believe the war is wrong.    The troops and the war are not
> the same thing, and it is possible for honest, reasoning people to
> treat them differently.    

I'm not trying to start another debate over politics; and, I understand 
that the far lefties (Kerry, Kennedy, et. al.) don't speak for the 
majority of this country.  (Any recent election would tell you that! 
(sorry - that was a cheap shot...))  But I have a really hard time with 
people that say they "support the troops, but..." and after that "but" 
is something that is opposed to what the troops are doing.

Is it a grandiose way of saying, "Well, I hope our folks don't *die*, 
but other than that, they're completely wrong"?  There's certainly 
nothing honorable about hoping a fellow human being doesn't die - most 
people feel that way about most other people.  There's a big difference 
between hoping someone doesn't die and supporting them - and, if that's 
what they're saying, then I'd have to agree with Mr. Prager; the problem 
isn't a problem of patriotism, it's a problem of honesty.
```

```
The national elections were close enough to show that the country is
either closely divided or centrist.    If in a close election one
party's candidates screw up badly, then that could be evidence that
had the two candidates been close in political ability then the voters
would have gone the other way.

banking infrastructure there.   After the war destroyed it.

Just because Bin Laden is delighted that we attacked Iraq doesn't mean
it was a bad thing.   And just because President Bush wanted to attack
Iraq doesn't mean it's a good thing.

At any rate, the war was started and we don't have any choice now -
but we can support our military in their effort to do their best under
war conditions.
```

```
"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
news:987f3$43f3d860$45491d7a$23049@KNOLOGY.NET...

> A lot of that praise is phony.  "I support the troops, but..."  There's no 
> room for "but"s - a person either supports the troops (and all that 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://www.townhall.com/opinion/columns/dennisprager/2005/07/12/154883.html

I too looked at that link, and I don't agree with his reasoning.

I would have to comment that most of those who self-identify as 
conservatives would regard me rather left of center for my social views, 
though I tend toward economic conservatism -- a combination which, if I 
recall correctly, the estimable Mr. Limbaugh claims makes me neither liberal 
nor conservative but rather fundamentally dishonest.

As to Mr. Prager's first comment, that the war in Iraq is "the wrong war in 
the wrong place at the wrong time", and that that sort of attitude is 
fundamentally incompatible with one of support for our troops.  I don't have 
a real opinion as to whether, in the cosmic scheme of things, this is the 
right war, or the right place, or the right time; I am not *yet* convinced 
that the reasons we put ourselves there were the right ones, however, and I 
hold our Commander-in-Chief, *alone* within the military hierarchy, 
accountable (either way) for that.  But we need to finish what we started, 
because slinking away with our national tail between our legs does not serve 
the well-being of Iraq, the United States, or the rest of the world. 
Failure to follow through does fundamental harm.  Once we've started 
swinging that "big stick", dropping it is a Really Bad Idea.

As to his second, a justification that war *is* sometimes the answer, citing 
several examples, I actually agree with him there -- though I think 
preserving *half* the Korean peninsula is illustrative of failure to follow 
through as completely as perhaps we might have.

His third comment is about opposition to military recruitment on major 
college campuses.  Most of the objection to military recruiting on campuses, 
as I understand it, is the military's official policies that discriminate 
against gay men and Lesbians as worthy candidates for the military.  The 
military perspective about gay people is fundamentally in violation of laws 
and policies that forbid discrimination in hiring on the basis of sexual 
orientation, in locations in which such laws and policies apply.

Whether the *real* reasons for which the war was initiated were wrong-headed 
or not (and just to be clear, I am *fundamentally* distrustful of claims of 
pure motives!), we're in it.  Now is not then.  I believe *very* strongly 
that it would make everything both in the US and abroad *much* worse for our 
leadership, and thus for us as a nation, to say "We made an oopsie, and 
we're going to go home now.".   Really, really, really bad idea.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 28)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>

```
 

"Peter Lacey" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:43EF81EC.BD753329@mts.net...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>
OK :-) I wasn't being sharp when I asked how it could happen, but I see how 
it could be interpreted that way. I am not trying to defend a position here 
because I really don't care whether people use it or not. I'm trying to 
explain it simply because it is different to the norm and I find it very 
successful. I wouldn't even raise this subject unless somebody asked me. I 
did a long time ago; until I learned better and did my own thing and let CLC 
get on with it... :-)
>> >
>> > Let me be more general, then.  Suppose a component starts giving
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I understand why you have a problem with this. (I was a procedural COBOL 
programmer for over 20 years.) Like I said it is a DIFFERENT approach.

I really don't know how I can explain this in a way that will enable you to 
see past what you "know"....

There is an old story about an archer who was an acknowledged master of the 
craft. When asked how he could possibly be so good with a bow and arrow he 
replied: "I claim that whatever the arrow hits is what I was aiming at..."

This is a bit like that, inasmuch as the functionality is developed (evolved 
by interaction and iteration, really) and seen to be working. Then it is 
documented as doing that.


>> 2. IF, by some incredible stretch of the imagination, and purely for the
>> sake of intellectual argument, the scenario you posit was to happen, no, 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> have posited it if it hadn't happened to me.

If you have never developed and written a component based system, then it 
hasn't happened to you. It has happened to you when you have inherited or 
developed standard code. I keep saying it is not the same, but the message 
doesn't seem to be getting through. Yes, both approaches involve writing 
code to program a computer. But that is where the similarities end. The 
methodology used to decide what to write, the units in which the code is 
written, evolution rather than design, the factors that decide when code is 
"finished" (goals are achieved), the way in which problems are fixed,  the 
methods of user interface and documentation, are all different from the 
usual approach.

I think it is better, but I'm not here to evangelise. I use it. It works for 
me. I defy anyone to find a post from ME complaining about having to 
maintain source code at 2am...:-)

>  I can think of three
> instances: all were programs executed at year-end or infrequently; all
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> impossible the default action was inappropriate.

Sure, I've had similar experiences, and I'm sure many people reading this 
have too. I haven't had such experiences since I started using component 
based design.

The inflation one is interesting... I would agree that if the fields 
overflowed because the computer could not accommodate them, then that would 
certainly be a flaw in either approach. In the component case it might 
involve an override of a property when we were dealing with currencies like 
lira or yen. Personally, I make accumulation fields the maximum size 
available on the platform (but that's just me, and some would argue it is an 
unnecessary inefficiency.)  It would be wise to trap overflow and ensure 
action was taken right at step one when the code was being developed.

Such a thing could conceivably slip through, though and the code would be 
broken in either case (whether you used components or you didn't.)  Because 
of encapsulation in a component based approach it is very easy to identify 
and fix the error with minimal effect on the existing code.  Even if you 
didn't have the source code, you could STILL fix it in a component based 
system. (You would need to wrap and override the offending method, and the 
Class would need to have been written in a manner that allows you to do this 
(implementing an interface or an extension or using factory) ) This is 
extreme and to do this simply to avoid changing the source (assuming you had 
it) would probably be pushing things unnecessarily. So, yes, in a case like 
this you might change the code. I can honestly say I haven't had such a 
case. I certainly don't consider this to be "routine maintenance" and I 
stand by the position that things like possible overflow should have been 
considered on day one when the code was written.
>
> Other causes are possible.  I'd say that nearly all of them are due to
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
It isn't arrogance that makes me say it, and you are pushing my position 
beyond where it was stated to go.

There is no hubris in saying "code does what it does". It is a simple 
statement of fact.

As you may have seen in other discussions here,  I do not believe in 
absolutes either.  I am saying the code does what it is documented to do. 
(Because the document was written AFTER the code was seen to be working...) 
It does it day in and day out for years on end.  Just like your operating 
system, your compiler, and most of the critical code in critical 
systems.(Many of which are component based...)  I am not saying what you 
assert above. I am happy to write code that hasn't crashed yet, and to know 
there is fair chance that it won't and I can run it with confidence. If and 
when it does, fine, I'll deal with that. But I won't be maintaining source 
code which generates more possible errors and increases the chances of it 
happening.

It is "over maintained" source that is most error prone, and yet, knowing 
this, there are still people preaching the value of maintaining source. For 
most of them it is because they have no other choice. I'm saying there ARE 
other choices, but it is easier  for many (particularly experienced 
programmers) to decide they are just a re-invention of something already 
understood, than it is to actually approach them anew, and evaluate them on 
their own merits, instead of  how they stack up to "what we do now".

I am not trying to "sell" components to you. I have no vested interest here. 
I really don't care whether anyone else uses them or not. I attempted to 
answer some questions, that is all.

>>
>> The problem we are having here is that you are trying to relate my 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Sorry, I fail to grasp the significance of that.

> The problem we are having here is that you are implying something that I
> consider to be incredible if not impossible: that code can be written
> and tested so perfectly in the beginning that maintenance is not
> necessary.

<Sigh...> That is not my position. I am saying that if you code "ADD 1 TO 
COUNT" and it compiles and runs correctly, it is reasonable to assume it 
will continue to do so. That is what it does. So if you ever need to 
increment a counter...:-) Now, you might need something that increments in 
2s. Well, the above component is not what you need. It doesn't do that. You 
might as well say that the general ledger system is pointless because it 
won't do debtors. Hey, they're both posting to ledgers, runing balances and 
controls and producing audit trails...

On the other hand, when you sat down and coded ADD 1 TO  COUNT you might 
have thought to yourself it might be a good idea to check for overflow on 
COUNT, and permit values other than 1 to update it... so,  now it becomes 
ADD INCREMENT TO COUNT ON OVERFLOW CALL COUNT-ERROR-HANDLER
The Help File [spec.] for the component now changes to say that it adds any 
number (placed in factory at beginning of run or day or whatever, or set as 
a property before invoking the "increment" method) to a counter and checks 
that the operation succeeded. If it didn't an error processing component is 
invoked.

So, if I need to increment a count...:-)

> I would venture a guess that it's not impossible that there
> are others following this "ng" that agree with me.

I would accept that there are many (probably a majority) here who have never 
used Object Oriented programming or built a component based system. That 
would tend to make your statement very likely.

In no other way can
> I take your suggestion that nobody will do maintenance of code as
> meaningful.

Do you normally maintain code that is correct? No, you maintain code because 
it is wrong. If it works, you leave it alone. :-)

Sometimes, circumstances will change and, because the bit that needs to be 
changed is embedded with other good code that doesn't need to be changed, 
you change the "bad" bits and regression test the lot. Components give you a 
much better chance of only needing to change or replace the bad bits; 
everything is much more decomposed into encapsulated specific functionality. 
Lego bricks; not concrete slabs (and, yes, I accept there are places where 
concrete slabs are very useful. However, for the most part, commercial 
computer programming is not about building motorways; it is about holiday 
villas and  comfortable, purpose built homes and offices...) You don't need 
to regression test the good bits because they HAVEN'T changed.


 With respect, I can't get away from decades of experience:
> things will change over the course of time that will require revisiting
> the code.  I am NOT questioning your methods of development.
>

That's really OK. I understand your feeling about it. I  can't explain it 
more than I already have and the "decades of experience" make it difficult 
to see. (That is not a criticism; I had exactly the same problem when I 
first embarked on this.)

Just ask yourself this: "If I HAD NO SOURCE CODE what would I do?"

I have live applications which use components for which I don't have source. 
I use Windows components every day for which I don't have source (so do you 
(if you run in the windows environment) but you are not aware of it).

It isn't a problem.

A component that cost a couple of hundred dollars could  save me months of 
work. (I have some that come in this category and I have others that I have 
traded that saved me heaps of work also.) These components get reused over 
and over and pay for themselves many times. If one of them DID fail (so far 
none of them ever has) I'd get a replacement from the guy who wrote it, OR 
I'd simply write a replacement myself. I have that option. The Methods, 
Properties, and Events are documented, along with the interfaces; replacing 
it would be a fairly simple task (in most cases; not the ones that would 
take months to write :-)) It is still better than trying to maintain source, 
in my opinion. The other thing abut this approach is that some of the people 
writing these components have specialist knowledge of certain areas, and 
produce components with methods and properties you might never think of if 
you did it yourself. (Yes, you could add them later, but it's much better if 
you don't have to :-)) I only have one lifetime; I cannot and do not desire 
to be, expert at everything.


>> >What you call a spec I'd call documentation,
>> > because it's post-facto.
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Hahaha! I take your point... :-)

Pete.
```

```
> The inflation one is interesting... I would agree that if the fields 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> being developed.

    I'm going to a different topic with this post...

    Java has this class (or "component", if you like) called BigInteger 
which allows you to represent arbitrarily large integers. Whereas in Java, 
the "int" data type can range from negative 2^31 to positive ((2^31)-1), 
BigIntegers are limited only by how much memory your computer has, so a 
machine with 2 GB of RAM could "almost represent" the integer 2^(number of 
bits in 2 GBs). I say almost, because there is some overhead to using the 
BigInteger class itself, so you don't get to use the whole 2 GBs.

    If we took every location where we used the "int" data type and replaced 
it with "BigInteger", there would "never" be overflow errors again (subject 
to how much RAM one has available). The main reason this hasn't been done 
yet is that compuation involving BigInteger is perceived to be slower than 
those involving only int.

    So before I had a job, I tried writing some benchmarks to see what kind 
of performance hits we actually get from using BigInteger. I had planned to 
write about 12 different benchmarks, but only got around to finishing one of 
them: Finding prime numbers using Eratosthenes' Sieve. The BigInteger 
version of the program was approximately 7 times slower than the one using 
int.

    7 times slower is a pretty big hit, but I figure in most business 
applications, integer arithmatic is not the bottleneck. If some operation in 
your application took 700 milliseconds instead of 100 milliseconds, would 
that be worth it in exchange for never having to worry about overflow again?

    Anyway, I kind of dropped this project once I got a job. Maybe I'll pick 
it up again in a few years.

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Something has to be tested and maintained was Re: GoTo in Java

_(reply depth: 21)_

- **From:** Clark Morris <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2006-10-28T02:18:44+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3gtuj2hgm7roiraii0526t4avd5p7t6a8h@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<dnnqs1tjutcc64coojpi0k06g2jrlopf38@4ax.com> <S9SBf.194761$OU5.25957@clgrps13> <43qn0fF1oqu69U1@individual.net> <F77Cf.110590$AP5.18493@edtnps84> <1138298486.064822.222920@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12> <1138390683.396793.322920@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <43vp1uF1pnsa5U1@individual.net>`

```
My comments on the following from months back are top posted with
nothing interspersed. As someone who is a modifier and reworker of
existing systems, I won't really learn or understand Java or OO until
I work with it which at my age may be never.  However, I strongly
believe that we in the field have failed to understand that scripts
are code, control parameters be they sort control statements or table
entries in a package are code and in fact anything used to control the
manipulation of data is code. The systems that you describe in several
of your postings scare me in many ways.  I hope that whatever tool or
tool set was used to create these applications with just drag and drop
combining of components also produced the documentation to say what
was done so that when the business changes some poor soul has a
fighting chance of figuring out where to make the change(s) to what
was done.  I also hope that the development tools contain adequate
means to handle the needed testing and not just unit testing. 

The use of control parameters, control tables, etc. is powerful and
allows the use of generalized packages such as operating system of
choice or affliction, SAP or Oracle Financials, word processing
packages, etc..  While some of these are bounded, others especially in
house systems have no checking on them.  Older portions of operating
systems can be equally vulnerable to loose change mechanisms as I can
testify from experience on IBM's MVS.  I have nothing against
languages and tools that operate at higher levels of abstraction.  At
least in theory they can be expressing things in ways that are closer
to the description of the process to be implemented.  The use of
documented components is also a good thing and something that I am
used to as a former systems programmer although not with some of the
niceties of the OO paradigm.  If these tools also are more
self-documenting, especially to non-programmers, that too is something
I applaud.  

However the idea of something being unchanging and never needing
upgrade is not in keeping with the world as I know it.  Determining
the pervasiveness of a change and whether it should affect all
processing is something that probably has not been well thought in the
past.  I am used to a mainframe environment where a subroutine change
is picked up by all users (COBOL programs compiled with the DYNAM
option binding CALLed routines at execution time rather than link time
if they are not nested programs) upon implementation.  This has
implications for retrospective processing.  The advantage is that an
organization wide change takes effect immediately.  The disadvantage
is that a changed routine is immediately available to all callers.  OO
does not change the underlying dilemma.  The question is whether you
want the invoker/caller to pick up a changed version when it issues
the initial invoke/call for an execution unit or if you want to use a
new name or parameter to make any changed behaviour explicitly
requested.
```

```
"Clark Morris" <cfmtech@istar.ca> wrote in message 
news:3gtuj2hgm7roiraii0526t4avd5p7t6a8h@4ax.com...
> My comments on the following from months back are top posted with
> nothing interspersed. As someone who is a modifier and reworker of
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> manipulation of data is code.

They are indeed. Congratulations on your insight.

> The systems that you describe in several
> of your postings scare me in many ways.

Not sure who 'you' is in the above sentence, but, in case it is me, I'll 
respond quickly...

Being scared by new approaches is a perfectly normal reaction; must of us 
are anxious when we move out of our comfort zones. I am not an Evangelist, 
preaching anything or trying to convert the masses. I'm not on commission, 
not trying to flog any particular software, and I really don't care any more 
whether people here take up what I'm saying.

For a decade now I have been advocating that COBOL people expand their skill 
sets, embrace OO, get to understand component technology, look again at 
traditional development methodology, make themselves more marketable and 
lever their COBOL knowledge into the 21st Century. Some have; most haven't. 
I feel sorry when I see people here watching their jobs disappearing 
overseas. But, it was predictable and it didn't happen overnight.

What I post here is based entirely on my own perceptions and experiences. It 
works for me. I try to explain why it works, in the hope that others can 
benefit, but I am finally coming to the conclusion that most of what I'm 
saying is simply being perceived through a fog of pre-judgement and 
measurement against 'what we've always done', rather than open-minded 
assessment and experiment.

I am on record as saying that maintenance of source code is self defeating. 
Instead of trying to grasp what I'm getting at (and I've spelled it out 
several times here) it has been easier for people to say: "Well, we've 
ALWAYS maintained source code. That's what COBOL is good for. How could you 
possibly maintain a system if you DIDN'T change the source code?" (the last 
sentence is rhetorical because 'everybody knows' maintenance is impossible 
without changing code...)

And yet, I have built systems that use functional encapsulation and object 
orientation to ensure that their methods (behaviours) and properties (public 
and private data) DON'T need to be changed. Some of these systems have been 
live for over 5 years now and have had functionality added and amended 
WITHOUT changing existing source code, and WITHOUT having to regression test 
every module or program when new functionality is added.

It CAN be done; it just can't be done if you continue the same approaches 
that have been used for the last forty years.

If you decide to change one room of a brick house do you grind up the 
existing bricks and make them into new bricks? Why would you do that? The 
existing bricks are fine, they just need to be organised differently and 
maybe some new ones added. Maybe you'll have some bricks left over from the 
previous design. Perhaps you can use these to build a barbeque... The point 
is that a brick is a brick; it has the attributes and behaviours of a brick. 
There is no point in trying to change an existing brick into a corner brick 
(although you CAN do so and some companies do this; I believe it defeats the 
purpose of using bricks in the first place). And once a brick is made, it 
remains a brick forever. Don't try and sharpen it or round it; make sharper 
or rounder bricks for that. (You will find it is easy to modify the existing 
mould for this)



>  I hope that whatever tool or
> tool set was used to create these applications with just drag and drop
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> was done.

Why is it always 'the poor soul' at 3 in the morning who has do maintenance? 
:-) Doesn't maintenance ever get done in working hours?

The tool sets I referred to DO generate documentation and they do it 
graphically and on-line. You can instantly see the structure of any given 
project and the relationships between the various objects. (You may have 
seen the diagrams generated by MS ACCESS if you click the 'Relationships' 
option... the principle is similar.)

Just coming back to the environment you are used to for a minute, when was 
the last time you went to a filing cabinet and retrieved a program listing 
so you could look at what it did? Not recently...?  No, we all learned 
pretty quickly that what was filed with the documentation was pretty useless 
for maintining the program. The current source stored in the system is the 
ONLY definitive documentation that is worth a damn. It is exactly the same 
with objects.

The processes I use for development ensure that the designs are documented 
as they are developed, and the system documents the objects, noting each 
method, property, and event, and the relationships between components. Help 
files for each Class are also built. There is no reason why an OO system 
should be any less 'documented' than a non-OO one, but the emphasis should 
be on USEFUL documentation, rather than documentation for the sake of having 
somethig in a filing cabinent.




> I also hope that the development tools contain adequate
> means to handle the needed testing and not just unit testing.
>

Naw... debug it in live... that's my motto... :-)

> The use of control parameters, control tables, etc. is powerful and
> allows the use of generalized packages such as operating system of
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> I applaud.

So why does it scare you?

>
> However the idea of something being unchanging and never needing
> upgrade is not in keeping with the world as I know it.

Absolutely. The observable universe is apparently dynamic. No less so when 
it comes to business...

The question is not whether things need to change; they do. The question is 
HOW to manage those changes.

Personally, I don't think that altering procedural code is an ideal way to 
do it. The act of altering it introduces more chance of error. Just because 
that was the only option for several decades doesn't mean that it is right. 
Now we have alternative choices. We can encapsulate functionality into 
fundamental units (components). They do what they do. Ask a radio engineer 
about the last time he modified a given pcb or chip... He didn't. Everything 
is modular and encapsulated... even the integrated stuff...input, process, 
output... component technology analogised at an engineering level. (Software 
engineering could do well to look at electrical engineering...)


> Determining
> the pervasiveness of a change and whether it should affect all
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> implications for retrospective processing.

Yes, it does. Even though it is probably the best approach to managing that 
particular environment.



> The advantage is that an
> organization wide change takes effect immediately.  The disadvantage
> is that a changed routine is immediately available to all callers.  OO
> does not change the underlying dilemma.

It depends on how you implement the OO. It CAN change the underlying dilemma 
if you implement it so that there are no changes to any existing interfaces. 
No changed parameter lists; everything calls what it always did, in exactly 
the way that it always did. The new functionality is simply a new method 
call.

Although invoked objects CAN have parameters passed to them, I don't 
personally do this. Instead, I use GET and SET on the properties before (and 
after) invoking the method I want.

It would take too long to go into the subtle details of why this is 
important, here, but the main benefit is that if interfaces don't change, 
then regression testing can be avoided. New functionality is turned on by 
new properties being set; the same method works exactly as it did before, 
for the cases where these properties are NOT set... Previous code executes 
exactly as it did previously (it has not been amended), but new code can use 
the same method to achieve a different result by setting properties before 
it is invoked. The new code can be added as a new method that wraps the 
existing method so that code and functionality is not duplicated. The 
details and examples are more than I am prepared to post here, but I'll 
gladly help anyone who's interested, if they contact me.



>  The question is whether you
> want the invoker/caller to pick up a changed version when it issues
> the initial invoke/call for an execution unit or if you want to use a
> new name or parameter to make any changed behaviour explicitly
> requested.

Exactly. See above.

Some further comments after the interesting discussion on code translation 
below...
>
> On Sat, 28 Jan 2006 12:35:21 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>

It's a bit like saying: "Don't vote; the Government will get in..."

Procedural source is procedural source and it really doesn't matter what 
language you translate it into. The problem is not with the language, it is 
with the paradigm.

>>I've been following this thread with some interest and I understand what
>>Oliver is trying to do. It is a noble idea, but I think Richard has hit 
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

Can't believe I wrote that...:-) (It was a NZ Government slogan many years 
ago, probably buried in my subconconscious...nevertheless, I stand by what I 
wrote above.)

>>Fortunately, modern evironments are so component rich (even though many
>>people do not realise the thousands of components that are provided free
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

OK, that's really everything I need/want to say about this... :-)

Disagree with me if you want (and I know many here do) but that is what I 
think, and I honestly believe the next breakthrough will be 'smart 
components' that will come with pre-programmed concepts... But that's 
another story, and the sun is shining here and I don't have to work so  I'm 
off to the beach... :-)

Pete.
```

```
In article <0lsCf.112291$m05.1772@clgrps12>, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> writes:
> 
>     To prevent 20 page documentation for a 20 line block of code, the 
> comments could themselves be structured (perhaps via XML?) so you could 
> instruct your IDE to filter out the parts that are not relevant to you. 

Now you're getting into the realm of Literate Programming, which I
think is probably the direction you ought to go with this idea.
Understanding the intent of programs is a task for humans, not
software; so give it to the author, and not to a decompiler.  That's
not to say that decompilers couldn't assist with the sort of multiple-
language representation you're describing, but I think most of the
tooling would be on the front side, where the programmer is.

I think Literate Programming has a lot going for it, actually, but
the development environments (and not just IDEs - we don't all like
IDEs) need work.
```
